<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="2c677e08c6964e0f9f7eaff8f293c6c0"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2c677e08c6964e0f9f7eaff8f293c6c0">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'e5955e10-0844-4ef7-992e-795fb5cd00fb'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d60622489" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>Redox-active tri(dithiolylidene)cyclohexanetrione centers have been successfully integrated into a series of covalent organic frameworks with two-dimensional crystalline structures, excellent stability, high porosity, and good conductivity, and rich Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites, making them suitable anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/773348de-aa8a-456f-a57d-16da76593a62/anie202417493-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/773348de-aa8a-456f-a57d-16da76593a62/anie202417493-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7debc8b2-8145-49cc-9ba6-cba4e9716fa1/anie202417493-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/773348de-aa8a-456f-a57d-16da76593a62/anie202417493-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d60622492" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The rational design of porous covalent organic frameworks (COFs) with high conductivity and reversible redox activity is the key to improving their performance in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, we report a series of COFs (FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF, and FPDC-TPT-COF) based on an organosulfur linker, (trioxocyclohexane-triylidene)tris(dithiole-diylylidene))hexabenzaldehyde (FPDC). These COFs feature two-dimensional crystalline structures, high porosity, good conductivity, and densely packed redox-active sites, making them suitable for energy storage devices. Among them, FPDC-TPT-COF demonstrates a remarkably high specific capacity of 420 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>), excellent cycling stability (~87 % capacity retention after 3000 cycles, 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and high rate performance (339 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>) as an anode for SIBs, surpassing most reported COF-based electrodes. The superior performance is attributed to the dithiole moieties enhancing the conductivity and the presence of redox-active carbonyl, imine, and triazine sites facilitating Na storage. Furthermore, the sodiation mechanism was elucidated through in situ experiments and density functional theory (DFT) calculations. This work highlights the advantages of integrating multiple functional groups into redox-active COFs for the rational design of efficient and stable SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417493-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417493-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The rapid increase of hybrid electric vehicles and portable electronic devices necessitates the development of reliable rechargeable battery technologies with accessible resources and high energy density.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0001_R_d60622481e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Despite the significant advancements in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in recent years, the scarcity, uneven distribution, and rapid consumption of Li resources have raised concerns about their sustainability and cost.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0004_R_d60622481e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> In this context, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a promising complementary technology due to the high natural abundance of sodium resources.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0007_R_d60622481e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> However, the larger radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) compared to Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å) results in sluggish electrode kinetics and a serious volume change in the electrode materials, presenting a formidable challenge in developing high-rate and stable SIB anodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0011_R_d60622481e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> To date, many anode materials have been developed for SIBs, such as transition metal oxides (Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0015_R_d60622481e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nature">5</a></sup></span> Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0016_R_d60622481e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Nano">6</a></sup></span> MnO,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0017_R_d60622481e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span> CuO,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0018_R_d60622481e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span> NiO,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0019_R_d60622481e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Nano">9</a></sup></span> etc.), polyanionic compounds (NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0020_R_d60622481e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">10</a></sup></span> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0021_R_d60622481e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Commun.">11</a></sup></span> Na<sub>3</sub>(TiOPO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0022_R_d60622481e584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Eng. J.">12</a></sup></span> etc.), and organic anode materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0023_R_d60622481e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> However, several issues remain unresolved. Most transition metal oxides and small organic molecules exhibit poor long-term stability because of corrosion or dissolution in electrolytes during the charge/discharge process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0027_R_d60622481e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> Although polyanionic compounds can overcome the dissolution problem, their low porosity limits mass transport and causes swelling/shrinkage during the galvanostatic charge/discharge process, which severely shortens the device lifetime.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0031_R_d60622481e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span> Therefore, exploring new types of electrode materials is highly desirable.</p>
         
         <p>Covalent organic frameworks (COFs) are an emerging class of highly ordered crystalline porous materials constructed through the covalent linkage of organic building blocks.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0032_R_d60622481e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16">16</a></sup></span> Benefiting from their unique structural features and physicochemical properties, COFs have exhibited great potential in metal-ion batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0036_R_d60622481e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17">17</a></sup></span> First, the well-defined and designable micropores of COFs provide continuous open channels for Na<sup>+</sup> transfer with minimal volume expansion, improving the Na<sup>+</sup> transport and rate capabilities. Second, the rigid and insoluble extended frameworks of COFs guarantee the stability of the electrode materials in the electrolyte, thus enhancing the cycle durability of the battery. More importantly, the diversity of building blocks and the atomically controllable structures of COFs allow for the accurate design of multiple functional groups as redox-active sites, optimizing the Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance of electrode materials. Although several COFs have been reported as electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries, most of them suffered from limited capabilities due to their sparse active sites and low electrical conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0044_R_d60622481e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18">18</a></sup></span> Therefore, COFs with densely packed redox-active sites, high stability, and good conductivity are highly desirable for improving the electrochemical performance of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Tri(dithiolylidene)cyclohexanetrione (DC) can be regarded as a derivative of tetrathiafulvalene (TTF), exhibiting redox activity and electrical conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0048_R_d60622481e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Org. Chem.">19</a></sup></span> Compared to TTF, the DC unit contains additional carbonyl groups, resulting in enhanced redox-active properties.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0049_R_d60622481e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Commun.">20</a></sup></span> Notably, TTF-based porous materials have been widely used in energy storage devices and photocatalytic.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0050_R_d60622481e625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21">21</a></sup></span> Considering the structural and physicochemical similarities between DC and TTF, integrating DC centers into COFs offers a promising platform for efficient Na<sup>+</sup> charge storage.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we synthesized a series of COFs containing DC centers as anodes to achieve stable and high-rate SIBs. To realize this, we initially designed a DC-based linker, namely, (trioxocyclohexane-triylidene)tris(dithiole-diylylidene))hexabenzaldehyde (FPDC). Subsequently, FPDC was linked by tris(aminophenyl)amine (TPA), 1,3,5-tris(4-aminophenyl)benzene (TPB), and 2,4,6-tris(4-aminophenyl)-1,3,5-triazine (TPT) linkers into FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF, and FPDC-TPT-COF, respectively. Benefiting from their redox activity, fairly good conductivity, high porosity, and chemical stability, the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode delivered excellent cycle stability and great rate capability. After 3000 cycles, it exhibited a high capacity retention of ~87 % and a reversible capacity of 312 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Even at a high current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, it still provided a stable electrochemical performance of 339 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, the sodiation of C=O and C=N bonds from redox-active carbonyl, imine, and triazine sites was elucidated through in situ experiments and density functional theory (DFT) calculations. This work not only opened a new avenue for designing multi-functional COFs as anode materials for SIBs but also provided insights into the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism in COF anodes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417493-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417493-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p><b>Design and synthesis of FPDC-based COFs</b>. Topological analysis provided a blueprint for the design of desired COF materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0056_R_d60622481e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Rev.">22</a></sup></span> We initially designed a DC-based linker containing six aldehyde groups, namely, FPDC, which can be regarded as a 6-connected building unit (Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0001">1a</a>). From a topological perspective, the 6-connected building units can be extended through 3-connected building units to form a 2D network with the <b>kgd</b> topology.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0057_R_d60622481e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">23</a></sup></span> To realize this design, TPA, TPB, and TPT were chosen as the 3-connected building blocks. The Schiff base reaction between FPDC and TPA (TPB or TPT) gave rise to the expected COFs, namely, FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF, and FPDC-TPT-COF (Figures <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0001">1b</a>-1d). Experimentally, these three COFs were synthesized by solvothermal reactions in a mixture of o-dichlorobenzene/<i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylacetamide in the presence of acetic acid (6 M) at 120 °C for 7 days, which afforded orange microcrystalline solids.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417493-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6f2bf5c4-556b-450b-a7b4-1de31c6dd684/anie202417493-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6f2bf5c4-556b-450b-a7b4-1de31c6dd684/anie202417493-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c577a09b-11a8-4028-a860-436ec5956887/anie202417493-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6f2bf5c4-556b-450b-a7b4-1de31c6dd684/anie202417493-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417493-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417493" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Schematic representation of the synthesis of FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF, and FPDC-TPT-COF. Structures of (b) FPDC-TPA-COF, (c) FPDC-TPB-COF, and (d) FPDC-TPT-COF. Hydrogen atoms are omitted for clarity.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p><b>Structural characterization</b>. The crystalline structures of FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF, and FPDC-TPT-COF were characterized by powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) measurements combined with structural simulations and Pawley refinement. Considering the geometry and connectivity of the molecular building blocks, structural models of these COFs were constructed in the trigonal <i>P-3</i> space group. Taking FPDC-TPA-COF as an example, it exhibits an intense peak at 3.75° and two minor peaks at 6.34° and 7.23°, which are attributed to reflections from the (100), (1–20), and (200) planes, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0002">2a</a>). The PXRD patterns of FPDC-TPB-COF (Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0002">2b</a>) and FPDC-TPT-COF (Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0002">2c</a>) are similar to that of FPDC-TPA-COF, but their diffraction peaks appear at lower angles, indicating increased unit cell lengths. Moreover, the Pawley-refined PXRD patterns agreed well with the experimental results (Figures <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0002">2a</a>-2c), with <i>R</i>wp and <i>R</i>p values converging to 4.91 and 3.81 % for FPDC-TPA-COF, 3.71 and 2.89 % for FPDC-TPB-COF, and 4.43 and 3.27 % for FPDC-TPT-COF, respectively. The lattice parameters obtained by Pawley refinement are summarized in Tables S1−S3.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417493-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c0ebdb98-6266-4625-94f4-e740def70b66/anie202417493-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c0ebdb98-6266-4625-94f4-e740def70b66/anie202417493-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/510d6cf5-172f-4117-8c79-89f73be9ad05/anie202417493-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c0ebdb98-6266-4625-94f4-e740def70b66/anie202417493-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417493-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417493" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Structural characterization of FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF, and FPDC-TPT-COF. PXRD patterns of FPDC-TPA-COF (a), FPDC-TPB-COF (b), and FPDC-TPB-COF (c). (d) FTIR spectra of FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF, FPDC-TPT-COF, and the corresponding building blocks. (e) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption−desorption isotherms of FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF, and FPDC-TPT-COF. (f) PXRD patterns showing the stability of FPDC-TPT-COF. TEM and elemental mapping of FPDC-TPA-COF (g), FPDC-TPB-COF (h), and FPDC-TPT-COF (i); scale bar=200 nm.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The successful formation of FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF and FPDC-TPT-COF was further verified by Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy and solid-state NMR (ssNMR) spectroscopy. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0002">2d</a>, the N−H stretching band at 3200 cm<sup>−1</sup> almost disappeared, and a new peak at 1620 cm<sup>−1</sup> was observed for FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF and FPDC-TPT-COF, confirming the formation of imine linkages. The FTIR spectra of FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF and FPDC-TPT-COF exhibit a peak at 1627 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can be assigned to the stretching vibration of carbonyl groups (C=O). In addition, the peaks at 158 ppm in the ssNMR spectra of FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF and FPDC-TPT-COF further verified the existence of imine bonds (Figure S2). To evaluate the porosity of FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF and FPDC-TPT-COF, nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms were collected at 77 K. All three COFs show type I isotherms, indicating microporous structures (Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0002">2e</a>). Accordingly, the Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) specific surface areas were estimated to be 901 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for FPDC-TPA-COF, 786 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for FPDC-TPB-COF, and 1057 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for FPDC-TPT-COF. The pore size distribution was also calculated for FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF, and FPDC-TPT-COF using the DFT model. They all possessed uniform major micropores of approximately 1 nm, matching well with the rhombic channels in the structural models (Figure S3).</p>
         
         <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images show that the morphologies of FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF and FPDC-TPT-COF are block nanoparticles with an average size of 200 nm (Figure S4 and Figures <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0002">2g</a>-2i). Additionally, the corresponding elemental mapping analysis under TEM reveals that N, S and O are uniformly distributed over FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF and FPDC-TPT-COF, suggesting a uniform composition throughout the materials. Thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA) demonstrates that these COFs are stable up to 380 °C under air without significant weight loss (Figure S5). To investigate the chemical stability of these COFs, we immersed them in various organic solvents, including <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide (DMF), EtOH, EA, DCM, PE, boiling water, HCl (0.5 M), and NaOH (10 M), for 24 h. The PXRD data and FTIR data after treatment in these solvents revealed that these COFs retained their crystallinity upon these treatments (Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0002">2f</a>, Figure S6, and Figure S7).</p>
         
         <p>The optical properties of these COFs were analyzed using ultraviolet/visible (UV/Vis) diffuse reflectance spectroscopy, revealing their strong absorption in the visible light region. (Figure S8). As revealed by the Tauc plot, the optical band gaps of FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF, and FPDC-TPT-COF are 2.1, 2.2 and 2.2 eV, respectively (Figure S9). We further investigated the electrical conductivity of FPDC and these COFs (Figure S10 and Table S4). The electrical conductivity of FPDC, FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF and FPDC-TPT-COF were measured to be 5.62×10<sup>−4</sup>, 1.19×10<sup>−5</sup>, 1.01×10<sup>−5</sup>, and 9.16×10<sup>−6</sup> S m<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The electrical conductivity of FPDC-based COFs are higher than traditional COFs without organosulfur units (Table S5). This could be explained by the S⋅⋅⋅S contact between neighboring organosulfur DC units, which provides pathways for electron transfer.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0058_R_d60622481e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span> The high porosity, fairly good conductivity, chemical stability and dense redox-active functional groups of these COFs make them suitable candidates for SIB applications.</p>
         
         <p><b>Electrochemical performance</b>. The presence of abundant redox-active functional groups (carbonyl, imine, and triazine units) along the microchannels channels demonstrate the potential of these COFs as anode materials in SIBs. Therefore, we investigated the electrochemical performance of COF-derived electrodes. As shown in Figures <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0003">3a</a>-3c and Figure S11, the galvanostatic charge–discharge profile of the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode in SIB provided the highest initial discharge capacity of 420 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> among the three COFs (262 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for FPDC-TPA-COF and 330 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for FPDC-TPB-COF). Obviously, for the three COFs electrodes, there is a certain decay of capacity in the first 10 cycles. This is due to the formation of solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI), which typically occurs during the initial cycles. The FPDC-TPT-COF electrode demonstrated a remarkable initial reversible capacity of approximately 390 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over the first 10 cycles and maintained a capacity of 374 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles. In contrast, the SIBs based on FPDC-TPA-COF and FPDC-TPB-COF electrodes exhibited severe capacity degradation and retained a very limited capacity after cycling. The above results may be related to the triazine units of the FPDC-TPT-COF as Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites. It is worth noting that the charge/discharge platform is not conspicuous in the galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles. This is attributed to the fact that the redox centers are not electronically isolated in the materials. Instead, electron coupling between neighboring redox centers caused the redox potentials of the redox centers in these COF electrodes to gradually shift during charge and discharge, resulting in a sloping cell voltage without obvious plateau.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0059_R_d60622481e823" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Rev.">25</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the rate capability of the three COF-derived electrodes was further evaluated (Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure S12). The FPDC-TPT-COF electrode delivered the highest discharge capacities of 375, 370, 360, and 351 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Even at a high current density of 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode still exhibited a promising reversible capacity of 339 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. As the current density decreased to 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity of the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode reached 369 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. In contrast, the SIBs based on other COF-based electrodes exhibited poor rate performance.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417493-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5a7921c2-98d9-4793-8760-f623866a4c8f/anie202417493-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5a7921c2-98d9-4793-8760-f623866a4c8f/anie202417493-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/58fcad82-887d-4d21-a159-1559416a0098/anie202417493-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5a7921c2-98d9-4793-8760-f623866a4c8f/anie202417493-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417493-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417493" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical properties of sodium-ion batteries with various COF-based electrodes. (a–e) Charge-discharge profiles of the COF-based electrodes after 250 cycles at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (d) Rate performance of COF-derived electrodes at various current densities excluding the first cycle data points. (e) Cycling stability-retention of the discharge capacity at a current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (including the first discharge cycle) and Coulombic efficiency are plotted. (f) Performance comparison of FPDC-TPT-COF to previously reported SIB anodes. (g) The b-values determined from catholic current peaks. (h) Capacitive and diffusion contributions of FPDC-TPT-COF electrodes at various scan rates.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The long-term cycling stability is a pivotal factor for evaluating the performance of various COF-derived electrodes. At a current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the SIB with the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode exhibited an initial discharge capacity of 360 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and maintained 87 % capacity retention (312 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) even after undergoing 3000 charge-discharge cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0003">3e</a>). In sharp contrast, the two control COF-derived electrodes displayed a comparatively low discharge capacity (283, 238 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and capacity retention ratio (69 %, 46 %). The stability of the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode toward high electron accumulation and its rapid redox process at elevated current densities are truly remarkable. The excellent cycling stability of the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode could be ascribed to its inherent stability, which exhibited evident advantages in terms of specific capacity and cycle life compared to previously reported electrode materials (Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0003">3f</a> and Table S6). Additionally, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analysis showed that the impedance of charge transfer for the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode (109.8 Ω) was significantly lower than that of FPDC-TPA-COF (235.3 Ω) and FPDC-TPB-COF (165.1 Ω) after 3000 cycles (Figure S13 and Table S7), indicating a remarkable enhancement in electrode kinetics for FPDC-TPT-COF, facilitating the fastest Na<sup>+</sup>/e<sup>−</sup> transfer.</p>
         
         <p>To better understand the charge storage mechanism, electrode kinetics, and electrode stability, cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles of SIBs with various COF-derived electrodes were recorded within a potential range spanning from 0.15 to 3.0 V at a scan rate of 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure S14, during the cathodic scan in the first cycle, the CV curves of three COF-derived electrodes displayed a prominent reduction peaks at around 0.58 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, which can be ascribed to the formation of SEI.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0060_R_d60622481e899" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26">26</a></sup></span> In addition, two pairs of distinct redox peaks were observed, which can be attributed to the consecutive two-step sodiation/desodiation process. In the subsequent two cycles, the cathodic peak at around 0.58 V disappeared and the CV profiles overlapwell, indicating the high redox reversibility of three COF-derived anodes for SIBs. A comparison of the FPDC-TPA-COF and FPDC-TPB-COF electrodes revealed that sodium insertion occurred at approximately 1.3 V and 0.7 V during discharge, but the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode showed the largest current output and redox peak area. At the molecular level, the only structural difference between FPDC-TPT-COF and other COF-derived electrodes is the electron-deficient triazine ring throughout the nanochannel. This electron-deficient structure has a pronounced promotional effect on the sodiation process, facilitating the rapid attraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions to the nanopores of the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode. Furthermore, to gain profound insights into the rapid kinetics of the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode, CV measurements at various scan rates were conducted to identify the diffusion and capacitive contributions of the charge storage capacity (Figure S15). For each case, the CV of the sample subjected to 10 redox cycles was plotted. This was done to avoid the interference from the SEI formation, which happens during the initial cycles. Generally, the measured currents (i) at various scan rates follow a power law relationship with the scan rate (v): <i>i</i>=a<i>v</i><sup>b</sup>, where a and b are constants. When b is close to 0.5, it is a Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion-controlled process, while b ≈1 implies a surface capacitive-controlled process. As indicated in the aforementioned equation, the value of b can be determined by calculating the slope of the log(i)-log(v) plots. Figure S15 shows the CV curves with cathodic and anodic peaks. For the selected cathodic peak 1, the slope values for the FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF, and FPDC-TPT-COF electrodes were determined to be 0.73, 0.81, and 0.85, respectively, as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0003">3g</a>. The slope values showed that the charge storage mechanism in these COF-derived electrodes is a combination of diffusive and capacitive processes, indicating capacitance-dominated storage behavior. Moreover, further detailed calculations of the capacitive contributions showed that FPDC-TPT-COF exhibited the highest capacitance-dominated storage behavior, which is favorable for the fast transfer of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0003">3h</a> and Figures S16–19). Consequently, the excellent reaction kinetics, highly porous nature, and nitrogen-doped conjugated structure ensure that the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode exhibits a remarkable capacity and exceptional rate capability for sodium batteries.</p>
         
         <p><b>Mechanistic studies for sodiation/desodiation of the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode</b>. To gain deeper insights into the Na<sup>+</sup>-storage mechanism of this high-performance FPDC-TPT-COF anode during the electrochemical process, in situ FT-IR and ex situ XPS analyses were performed during the charge and discharge stages to monitor the transformation of functional groups and elucidate the evolution of redox-active moieties. Figures <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0004">4a</a>, <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0004">b</a> shows the in situ FT-IR spectra of the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode during the charge–discharge process. Before sodiation, the characteristic peaks for the carbonyl (C=O), imine and triazine (C=N) groups of the pristine FPDC-TPT-COF electrode could be observed at approximately 1627 (1533) cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1545 cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When discharging to 0.05 V for sodiation, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0004">4b</a>, the detected peaks for C=O and C=N almost disappeared, indicating the reduction of these groups along with sodiation. When charged to 3.0 V, the distinctive peaks of C=O and C=N progressively reappeared and finally returned to their pristine intensities, implying that the redox process of the C=O and C=N groups in the skeleton was reversible and synchronous during the sodiation/desodiation process.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417493-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ab0a6fe5-af4e-4228-8776-1a858689ed3e/anie202417493-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ab0a6fe5-af4e-4228-8776-1a858689ed3e/anie202417493-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/52f1c321-beb4-447f-adb1-eecfc21e600b/anie202417493-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ab0a6fe5-af4e-4228-8776-1a858689ed3e/anie202417493-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417493-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417493" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Structural evolution of the active sites on the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode during the sodiation/desodiation processes. a) Discharge and charge profiles of the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.05–3.0 V during the initial two cycles. b) In situ FTIR spectra collected at different states corresponding to a). c) C1s, d) O1s and e) Na1s XPS spectra of the FPDC-TPT-COF electrode at different charge/discharge states.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Furthermore, the ex situ XPS spectra in Figures <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0004">4c</a>-e revealed further information on the transformation of functional groups throughout the sodiation/desodiation process. The pristine FPDC-TPT-COF electrode exhibited two peaks at 287.9 eV and 287.1 eV in the C 1s XPS spectrum, corresponding to the C=O and C=N functional groups, respectively. When discharged to 0.1 V, the intensity of the C=O and C=N peaks decreased, while the intensity of the C−O (286.8 eV) and C−N (286.2) peaks increased. Upon recharging the battery to 3.0 V, the signals for the C=O, C=N, C−O, and C−N groups exhibited the opposite trend, and all the peaks in the C1s spectra were almost identical to those in the pristine state. Similarly, the O1s spectra revealed a reversible process, wherein the signals for C=O (532.8 eV) almost vanished upon sodiation and subsequently recovered after desodiation. Moreover, the signals for C−O−Na (531.1 eV) and Na KLL Auger (535.9 eV) emerged after sodiation and vanished thereafter. Furthermore, the changes in the peak intensity at 1070.9 eV in the Na 1s spectrum further confirm the sodium storage behavior via the surface-redox reaction described by C=O + Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>−</sup>↔C−O−Na. Observations from these XPS analyses reconfirmed that C=N and C=O redox active sites participated in the entire sodiation process almost simultaneously, which is in good agreement with the in situ FTIR results.</p>
         
         <p>To validate the redox-active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage in FPDC-TPA-COF, FPDC-TPB-COF, and FPDC-TPT-COF, the binding energy of Na<sup>+</sup> on different sites of COFs was determined by DFT calculations through the ORCA 5.0.4 program package (Figure S20).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0064_R_d60622481e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27">27</a></sup></span> As shown in Table S8, the carbonyl groups from FPDC units were the primary Na<sup>+</sup> binding site (E<sub>ads</sub>=−68.06 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>), and the imine moieties were the secondary sites (E<sub>ads</sub>=−66.90 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>). Notably, the binding free energy of Na<sup>+</sup> on the TPT group (E<sub>ads</sub>=−47.82 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) is greater than that on the TPB (E<sub>ads</sub>=−5.04 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) and TPA (E<sub>ads</sub>=−3.18 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) groups, in accordance with the experimentally observed better SIB performance of FPDC-TPT-COF than those of FPDC-TPA-COF and FPDC-TPB-COF. The sodiation/desodiation process in the FPDC-TPT-COF anode was further investigated through extended semiempirical tight-binding model calculations by the xtb packag.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417493-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417493-bib-0070_R_d60622481e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28">28</a></sup></span> The electrostatic potentials (ESPs) of FPDC-TPT-COF were calculated based on a repetitive unit. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417493-fig-0005">5</a>, the equivalent minima of the ESP regions are located around the oxygen atoms of carbonyl groups, nitrogen atoms of imine groups, and nitrogen atoms of triazine groups in FPDC-TPT-COF, indicating that these groups serve as redox-active centers for Na<sup>+</sup> storage. Moreover, the calculated lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) of the FPDC-TPT-COF molecule demonstrated the stabilization effect of both N and O atoms on Na<sup>+</sup> due to their excellent π-conjugation (Figure S21). These ESP minima corresponded to 15 Na<sup>+</sup> accommodation sites, which were divided into three steps. The first step is three Na<sup>+</sup> ions interacting with O atoms from the FPDC units. Subsequently, the electronegative N atoms from the imine group are capable of connecting to one Na<sup>+</sup> in a chelating form, resulting in the incorporation of six Na<sup>+</sup> ions per repetitive unit. Finally, six Na<sup>+</sup> ions are placed at the N atoms in the two triazine groups. Thus, fifteen Na<sup>+</sup> ions are accommodated in each repetitive unit. The theoretical Na<sup>+</sup> uptake determined by DFT calculations matches well with the experimental capacity.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417493-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/297f7a4a-d9bb-457e-9df7-82922e2b0e7b/anie202417493-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/297f7a4a-d9bb-457e-9df7-82922e2b0e7b/anie202417493-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d34910c2-3762-435f-877e-41659ce82edd/anie202417493-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/297f7a4a-d9bb-457e-9df7-82922e2b0e7b/anie202417493-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417493-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417493" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Mapped electrostatic potential (ESP) diagram of the FPDC-TPT-COF unit and FPDC-TPT-COF-15Na on the van der Waals surface.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417493-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417493-sec-0003-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have successfully constructed a series of COFs based on FPDC linkers, demonstrating their potential as promising building units for multi-functional COFs with both conductivity and electrochemical activity. FPDC-based COFs serve as highly efficient anode materials for SIBs, exhibiting high specific capacity and stability. This performance is attributed to the enhanced conductivity from dithiole moieties and the efficient Na storage facilitated by redox-active carbonyl, imine, and triazine sites. This study underscores the unlimited potential of COFs in the rational design of efficient and stable SIBs by incorporating multiple functional groups. We expect that this work will broaden the scope of electrode materials based on redox-active COFs and open new avenues for high-performance electrochemical energy storage.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
